        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.3c16484"><meta property="og:title" content="Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/acsami.3c16484.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Hard carbons (HCs), while a leading candidate for sodium-ion battery (SIB) anode materials, face challenges in their unfavorable sodiation kinetics since the intricate microstructure of HCs complicates the Na+ diffusion channel. Herein, a Hovenia dulcis-derived HC realizes a markedly enhanced high-rate performance in virtue of dual-functionalized Ca. The interlayer doped Ca2+ effectively enlarges the interlayer spacing, while the in situ-formed CaSe templates induce the formation of hierarchical pore structures and intrinsic defects, significantly providing fast Na+ diffusion channels and abundant active sites and thus enhancing the sodium storage kinetics. Achieved by the synergistic effect of regulation of intrinsic microcrystalline and pore structures, the optimized HC shows remarkable performance enhancements, including a high reversible capacity of 350.3 mA h g–1 after 50 cycles at 50 mA g–1, a high-capacity retention rate of 95.3% after 1000 cycles, and excellent rate performance (108.4 mA h g–1 at 2 A g–1). This work sheds light on valuable insight into the structural adjustment of high-rate HCs, facilitating the widespread utilization of SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/acsami.3c16484.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8a991a18b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8a991a18b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'f1ecd262-3b71-4974-96aa-0289c6fb60f6'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da8a991a18b325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8a991a18b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c16484" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90da8a991a18b325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Dual-Functionalized Ca Enables High Sodiation Kinetics for Hard Carbon in Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c16484", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/medium/am3c16484_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Hard carbons (HCs), while a leading candidate for sodium-ion battery (SIB) anode materials, face challenges in their unfavorable sodiation kinetics since the intricate microstructure of HCs complicates the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel. Herein, a <i>Hovenia dulcis</i>-derived HC realizes a markedly enhanced high-rate performance in virtue of dual-functionalized Ca. The interlayer doped Ca<sup>2+</sup> effectively enlarges the interlayer spacing, while the in situ-formed CaSe templates induce the formation of hierarchical pore structures and intrinsic defects, significantly providing fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels and abundant active sites and thus enhancing the sodium storage kinetics. Achieved by the synergistic effect of regulation of intrinsic microcrystalline and pore structures, the optimized HC shows remarkable performance enhancements, including a high reversible capacity of 350.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a high-capacity retention rate of 95.3% after 1000 cycles, and excellent rate performance (108.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>). This work sheds light on valuable insight into the structural adjustment of high-rate HCs, facilitating the widespread utilization of SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D2397%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYing%2BLi%252C%2BJing%2BShi%252C%2BFeng%2BWu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D2%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c16484%26title%3DDual-Functionalized%2BCa%2BEnables%2BHigh%2BSodiation%2BKinetics%2Bfor%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D2407%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291356&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Defects" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Defects" class="article__tags__link">Defects</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Genetics" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Genetics" class="article__tags__link">Genetics</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292229&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Morphology" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Morphology" class="article__tags__link">Morphology</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon+Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon%20anodes">hard carbon anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Interlayer+Spacing+Regulation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interlayer%20spacing%20regulation">interlayer spacing regulation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Pore+Engineering&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_pore%20engineering">pore engineering</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodiation+Kinetics&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodiation%20kinetics">sodiation kinetics</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Secondary batteries have earned great consideration to meet the escalating demand for energy storage technologies. Among these secondary batteries, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have gained prominence due to their remarkable energy density and cycle life. However, the constrained supply of lithium resources leads to its high cost, which hinders the widespread use of LIBs in large-scale energy storage systems (LSESS). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Conversely, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) serve as a promising alternative battery system to be applied in LSESS because of their abundant raw material reserves as well as low price. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Unfortunately, suffering from the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> and weak interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and graphitic layers, traditional commercial graphite anodes fail to store Na<sup>+</sup> reversibly in ester-based electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> To enhance the reliability and broaden the applications, it is imperative to focus on the development of anode materials that combine cost-efficiency and high capacity for SIBs. Currently, carbon materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> metal oxides/sulfides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> and alloying materials <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> are developed as viable anode materials for SIBs. Among them, hard carbons (HCs) have gained significant attention as promising candidates for anode materials in SIBs due to abundant resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> More importantly, HCs are composed of typical nongraphitizable structures with randomly packed carbon layers, which possess large interlayer spacing to store Na<sup>+</sup>, contributing to the high reversible sodium storage capacity in HCs. However, the complicated microstructure of HCs inevitably leads to the hindered Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel, resulting in unsatisfying sodiation kinetics and an inferior rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To date, regulating the graphitic microcrystalline structure and pore structure is an effective approach to ameliorating the sodium storage kinetics of HCs. Tuning microcrystalline parameters, such as the length (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>), thickness (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>), and (002) interlayer spacing (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) of graphitic layers, can effectively facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> transfer and increase the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity, particularly in the low-voltage region. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16−18)</a> Optimizing carbonization temperature, introducing heteroatoms (e.g., N, S, and P) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a> and metal ions (e.g., Ca<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Mn<sup>2+</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> have been investigated to regulate the lattice spacing and graphitization level in HCs. Specifically, it is crucial to obtain a suitable <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> since large interlayer spacing causes low intercalation capacity, while a small one hinders Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Our group used K<sup>+</sup>-enriched biomass precursor to modulate the interlayer spacing of HCs to 0.40 nm, benefiting an impressive capacity of 314 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> More recently, HCs with atomic Zn-doping in which zinc acetate acts as a Zn precursor also exhibit expanded graphite regions (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub> = 0.408 nm), contributing to a significantly improved reversible capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> In addition, modifying the pore structure (e.g., porosity and pore size) is a potent strategy to promote sodiation kinetics since it can (1) tune the specific surface area (SSA), (2) elevate the intrinsic defect concentration that favors the adsorption behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>, and (3) serve as channels to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Furthermore, the plateau capacity of hard carbons can be effectively increased by a rational design of pore structure, such as closed-pore structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28−30)</a> For instance, ultramicropores prepared by the molten diffusion-carbonization method can offer extra Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites and serve as ionic sieves that accelerating fast diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> during the sodiation/desodiation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Besides, to maximize radical sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, a multistage pore structure (macropores and ultramicropores) was built and achieved excellent rate capability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Therefore, it can be expected that synergistic modulation of the microcrystalline and pore structures can significantly achieve the enhancement of rapid sodium storage kinetics. However, the introduction of heteroatoms and templating techniques are suffering from the complicated design of complex precursors as well as multistep synthesis, which increase cost and operational challenges. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> Hence, it is a challenge to facilely regulate microcrystalline and pore structure, achieving and maximizing the synergistic effect.</div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, we report a hierarchical porous biomass-based hard carbon that achieved rapid sodiation kinetics due to the dual-functionalized Ca. The simple one-step pyrolyzation was adopted to synchronously realize the formation of in situ CaSe templates and the regulation of the graphite microcrystalline structure. Besides, it is confirmed that the dual-functionalized Ca acts both as a dopant and template, thus enabling the synergistic control of the microcrystalline and morphological structure. The naturally doped Ca<sup>2+</sup> effectively expands the interlayer spacing of (002), and the in situ CaSe templates induce the formation of hierarchical pore structure (macropores and ultramicropores). This synergetic effect provides Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels, shortens the length of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion routes, and significantly facilitates electron transport, collectively improving sodium storage kinetics (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>). Therefore, the as-synthesized HC delivers a reversible capacity of 350.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with excellent rate ability (108.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and cycle stability (95.3% after 1000 cycles).</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/medium/am3c16484_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustration of Synthesizing Natural Ca<sup>2+</sup>-Rich HC</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>a,b, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>) illustrate the microstructures of the HCs produced at different carbonization temperatures and alkali treatments. It shows that the majority of HCs display micrograde particle size with a porous structure. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> presents a set of high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images, showing a significant trend in which the graphitization of the HCs undergoes a gradual increase with the carbonization temperature. HC-900 (hard carbon obtained at 900 °C) exhibits randomly oriented stripes in HR-TEM due to the lowest degree of lattice ordering. In contrast, HC-1200 (hard carbon obtained at 1200 °C) exhibits the highest lattice order, with more noticeable diffraction rings in the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) results. Besides, the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of the short-range ordered graphitized region (gradually decreases from 0.387 to 0.380 nm) also changes with the variation of carbonization temperature (from 900 to 1200 °C). Since the <i>Hovenia dulcis</i> precursor contains some natural heterogeneous atoms, to further identify the species and forms of existence of heterogeneous atoms in the HCs, energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) elemental mappings (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g–l) were performed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>g–i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a> show the presence of C, Ca, and Se in HC-1100 (hard carbon obtained at 1100 °C). The highly overlapping distribution regions of both Ca (1.14% of the atomic proportion) and Se (0.84% of the atomic proportion) suggest the existence of CaSe crystals with some free Ca<sup>2+</sup>. Besides, the TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c) provides a clear presence of CaSe. After the alkali treatment, the Se elements in the HC-1100A sample (HC-1100 treated with NaOH) are significantly decreased, while some Ca<sup>2+</sup> remains unremoved (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>k,l, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>). Meanwhile, the TEM result of HC-1100A shows the disappearance of agglomerated CaSe and the formation of macropores (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d). It is believed that the thermally evolved CaSe, which can be easily removed through alkaline treatment, acts as a template for pore formation. Moreover, there is little difference in the graphitization degree between the HC-1100 and HC-1100A, with no obvious <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> change (both are 0.382 nm, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e,f). Since the metal ions in the biomass precursor can serve as a buffer layer between the graphitic layers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref37" aria-label="reference 25 and 37">(25,37)</a> the interlayer Ca<sup>2+</sup> that is preserves after alkaline treatment can effectively extend <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of HCs.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/medium/am3c16484_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SEM images of (a) HC-1100 and (b) HC-1100A. The HR-TEM images of different samples: (c,e) HC-1100 and (d,f) HC-1100A. The SAED patterns of corresponding samples are displayed as insets of (c–f). The elemental distributions of (g,j) C, (h,k) Ca, and (i,l) Se for HC-1100 and HC-1100A samples, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A comprehensive exploration of the changes in the microcrystalline and morphological structures of the various HCs was employed by a combination of analytical techniques, including X-ray diffraction (XRD), Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET), and Raman spectroscopy. According to the XRD results, all samples suggested an amorphous/disordered structure and exhibited a broad diffraction peak at about 23° (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a), which can be attributed to the (002) peak of HCs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> With an increase in the carbonization temperature, the (002) peaks consistently undergo a shift toward larger angles. The corresponding <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> can be calculated according to Bragg equation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>)<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-5">2</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-6">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-7">sin</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-8" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-9">θ</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-10"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-11" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-12"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-13">n</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-14">λ</span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0" style=""></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>2</mn><mi>d</mi><mi>sin</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mi>θ</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>n</mi><mi>λ</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of the material exhibits a gradual reduction from HC-900 (0.387 nm) to HC-1000 (hard carbon obtained at 1000 °C, 0.385 nm), further decreasing from 0.382 nm in HC-1100 to 0.380 nm in HC-1200, demonstrating that high carbonization temperatures can promote the graphitization and reduce the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>, which corresponds to the previous report. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> To note, the change of <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> induced by alkali treatment is negligible (both are 0.382 nm), further indicating that the naturally doped Ca<sup>2+</sup> can remain in the interlayers after alkali treatment and enlarge <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>. In addition, a series of peaks at 26.0, 30.1, 43.1, 51.1, 62.7, 69.0, 71.1, 79.1, and 85.0° can be observed in the HCs without alkali treatment (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). These peaks can be indexed to the (111), (200), (220), (222), (400), (420), and (422) planes of the CaSe (JCPDS card no. 77-2012), respectively. It should be mentioned that the intensity of peaks of CaSe increases with temperature, demonstrating that CaSe is gradually formed by the thermal process. The disappearance of the CaSe peaks in HC-1100A further implies that CaSe can be effectively eliminated by the alkali treatment, which corresponds to the HR-TEM results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d). Moreover, the XRD results of HC-1100A showed merely the (002) and (101) peaks of hard carbon, and the TEM results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f) did not observe the lattice fringes of other items. Therefore, we believe that Ca<sup>2+</sup> mainly exists in the form of ions between the carbon layers.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/medium/am3c16484_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a,b) XRD patterns, (c) Raman spectra, (d) N<sub>2</sub> physisorption curves, and (e,f) pore size distribution curves of different HCs. XPS spectra of the HC-1100 and HC-1100A samples: (g) C 1s spectra, (h) Ca 2p spectra, and (i) Se 3d spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c shows the Raman spectra of HCs with different carbonization temperatures and alkali treatments. In all the Raman spectra, two prominent bands are evident, including the broad D-band (disordered carbon signal) that is positioned at 1345 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the relatively sharp G-band (graphitic carbon signal) situated at 1600 cm<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, the G-band signifies the sp<sup>2</sup> vibration of graphitic carbon, and the D-band indicates the sp<sup>3</sup> amorphous/defective phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The intensity ratio of the D and G bands (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) that can indicate the degree of graphitization <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref42" aria-label="reference 7 and 42">(7,42)</a> is calculated to be 1.11, 1.23, 1.17, 1.16, and 1.16 for the HC-900, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, and HC-1100A, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). Generally, the high temperature can promote the graphitization degree due to the increase of the carbon layer order and the reduction of porosity. The high <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of HC-1000 is due to the high disordered carbon layer and high defects induced by high porosity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Notably, the similar values of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> before and after the alkali treatment suggest that the alkali treatment induces little impact on graphitization, aligning with the TEM and XRD results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>c,d and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b).</div><div class="NLM_p">The porous structure of the HCs before and after alkali treatment was further illustrated by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption tests. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d–f, HCs are mainly characterized by the presence of ultramicropores (0.6 nm), which are generated by the decomposition of organic species. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> When the carbonation temperatures are 900, 1000, 1100, and 1200 °C, the SSA of the as-obtained HCs is 209.3, 571.7, 33.03, and 167.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. On account of the decomposition of organic species and abundant pore structure, HC-1000 exhibits a relatively high SSA. The subsequent decrease in SSA of HC-1100 is due to pore closure caused by high temperatures. HC-1200 has a relatively large SSA due to structural collapse at high carbonation temperatures, as shown in the SEM results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Additionally, the alkali treatment can remove in situ CaSe templates from the material and generate macropores (TEM results, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d), thus providing a relatively high SSA (46.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for HC-1100A). Therefore, HC-1100A possesses hierarchical pore structures (ultramicropores and macropores), in which ultramicropores can provide Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels and macropores can facilitate the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus contributing to sodium storage kinetics.</div><div class="NLM_p">The composition of surface functional groups on HCs was analyzed via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g–i). The C 1s spectra of different materials are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5</a>, where the proportion of C═C bonds increases with increasing temperature, indicating an increasing degree of graphitization. Three typical C 1s peaks of HC-1100 include C–C (284.8 eV, 34.37%), C–O (285.9 eV, 29.86%), and C═C bond (284.3 eV, 35.77%). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> HC-1100A presents three peaks containing C–C (284.7 eV, 36.27%), C–O (285.5 eV, 28.71%), and C═C bonds (284.2 eV, 35.02%). The higher ratio of C–C of 1100 A can be attributed to the exposed intrinsic defects in the hierarchical pore structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Furthermore, through a comparison of Ca 2p and Se 3d spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h,i), the content of Ca and Se of HC-1100A was reduced, demonstrating that CaSe was effectively removed by alkali treatment. Consequently, an HC material with dual-functionalized Ca has been proposed in which interlayer Ca<sup>2+</sup> can effectively expand the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> while the in situ CaSe templates act as a template to optimize the pore structure and increase intrinsic defects.</div><div class="NLM_p">To assess the electrochemical performance of HCs, cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) measurements were conducted. The CV curves at 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> of the HC electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6</a>. During the first cycle, there are two irreversible redox peaks at approximately 0.6 and 0.9 V that then disappear in the following cycles, attributed to the solid electrolyte interface generation during the first cycle. The cathodic peak at approximately 0.1 V represents the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> within the short graphitic layers, while the anodic peak (around 0.1 V) signifies the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The intensity of the two peaks shows the highest value in HC-1100 and HC-1100A since the suitable <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of HC significantly facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> storage. The CV results at the second and third cycles exhibit a high degree of overlap, demonstrating the remarkable reversibility of the HCs.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/medium/am3c16484_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical properties of different samples: (a) CV curves at 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> of the initial three cycles of HC-1100A; (b) GCD profiles of HC-1100A; (c) specific capacity from the plateau and slope contributions of HCs; (d) cycling performance at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (e) rate capability; (f) GCD curves at diversified current densities; and (g) cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> of HCs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The charge/discharge curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>) show a slope region (&gt;0.1 V), primarily associated with the surface adsorption/desorption of carbon materials. Additionally, a low-potential plateau region (&lt;0.1 V) signifies the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> To estimate the contribution of these two sodium storage behaviors to the overall capacity, the relative proportion of the plateau capacity and slope capacity was calculated. The contributions of the plateau capacity are 44.8, 34.1, 61.6, 58.9, and 58.0% for HC-900, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, and HC-1100A samples, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). Compared with HC-1100, HC-1100A demonstrates a higher ratio of slope capacity to total capacity. This can be attributed to the hierarchical pore structures along with the intrinsic defects generated by the CaSe templates. This distinctive structure can create active adsorption sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, thus leading to a pronounced increase in reversible capacity in the slope region. Besides, the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) differs across the samples, with values of 62.1, 64.1, 72.4, 66.4, and 72.1% for HC-900, HC-1000, HC-1100, HC-1200, and HC-1100A samples, respectively. The higher carbonization temperature can significantly promote the graphitization degree of HCs, consequently leading to reduced surface defect sites, optimized pore structure, and increased the ICE. However, when the temperature is further increased, the decrease in ICE can be ascribed to the irreversible and severe electrolyte decomposition caused by the structural collapse and the large SSA. Therefore, HC-1100 delivered the highest ICE. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, the specific capacities of the materials are 138.4, 204.1, 342.4, and 263.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when the carbonization temperature increases from 900 to 1200 °C, respectively. The increase in capacity with a higher carbonization temperature is mainly attributed to the improved graphitization, which facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Nevertheless, highly graphitized HCs provide less reversible specific capacity (263.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of HC-1200), suggesting that a small <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> is unfavorable for the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. It is concluded that a suitable <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> is necessary to favor the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Furthermore, HC-1100A exhibited a high specific capacity of 350.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> since the removal of CaSe templates can construct hierarchical pore structures and induce intrinsic defects that can improve the adsorption capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,f, the rate capabilities of different samples at different current densities are compared. The HC-1100A exhibits the most favorable rate performance, delivering specific capacities of 349.1, 302.5, 283.7, 244.1, 188.2, 146.0, and 108.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when the current densities are 20, 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Compared with previously reported HCs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 37, 49 and 50">(37,49,50)</a> HC-1100A exhibits a relatively higher rate performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). The excellent rate performance exhibited by the HC-1100A is believed to be associated with the abundant active defect sites provided by hierarchical pore structures and intrinsic defects, as well as the suitable microcrystalline structure, which facilitates rapid Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and storage. In addition, the long-term cycling performance of various HC samples was investigated at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g). After 1000 cycles, HC-1100A exhibited a high capacity retention of 95.3%, despite a gradual electrochemical activation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> In short, regulating the carbonization temperature and dual-functionalized Ca can effectively balance the graphitization degree, porosity, intrinsic defects, and <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of HCs, thus enhancing the sodium storage performance (e.g., plateau capacity and slope capacity ratio, ICE, and reversible capacity).</div><div class="NLM_p">The sodiation kinetics was investigated to clarify the underlying reasons for the significantly enhanced performance of HC-1100A. The CV curves scanned at different rates of HC-1100 and HC-1100A are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b, respectively. It can be observed that as the scan rate increases, the redox peak potential gap increases due to the polarization (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/suppl_file/am3c16484_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S8–S10</a>). The sodium storage behavior of HCs can be analyzed by the following equation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-15"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-16"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-17"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-18"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-19">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-20"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-21" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-22"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-23">a</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-24"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-25" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">v</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-26" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">b</span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>a</mi><msup><mi>v</mi><mi>b</mi></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where <i>i</i> represents the peak current (A). The variable <i>v</i> stands for the scan rate (mV s<sup>–1</sup>). <i>b</i> = 1 means a surface-controlled process, such as pseudocapacitance and electrical double-layer capacitance. When the <i>b</i> value closes to 0.5, it indicates a diffusion-controlled process, e.g., insertion/extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c, the <i>b</i> value at the peak of 0.01 V for HC-1100A is calculated as 0.56, suggesting a combined contribution from both diffusion-controlled and surface-controlled processes. The capacitive contribution of HC-1100 and HC-1100A can be further calculated by the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-27"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-28"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-29"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-30"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-31">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-32"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-33" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-34"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-35"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-36" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-37" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-38">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-40"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-41" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-42" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">2</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-43"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-44" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">v</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-45" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-46">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-47">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-48">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><msup><mi>v</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> correspond to surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled behaviors, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> According to the calculations, the HC-1100A anode delivers a higher capacitive contribution (64.63%) than HC-1100 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d–f). It can be concluded that hierarchical pore structures and intrinsic defects caused by removing CaSe templates can effectively motivate the capacitive behavior of HCs, thus improving the sodiation kinetic performance.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/medium/am3c16484_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. CV curves of (a) HC-1100 and (b) HC-1100A at different scan rates. (c) Linear relationship between the current and scan rate. The surface-controlled capacitive contribution of (d) HC-1100 and (e) HC-1100A at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled contribution ratios at various scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c16484/asset/images/large/am3c16484_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c16484&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was adopted for an in-depth evaluation of the charge transfer properties of HCs. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, the EIS profiles of different HCs consist of a semicircle observed in the high frequency related to charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and a linear region in the low frequency predominantly controlled by Warburg diffusion impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>). Notably, an equivalent circuit was used to quantify the resistance of the electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, and <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> values (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The fitting results show that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of HC-1100A (2.38 Ω) is smaller than HC-1100 (6.50 Ω), indicating a faster charge transfer process in HC-1100A. Besides, the Na-ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can be calculated through <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eqs 4">eqs 4</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a> as follows<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math
